<div id="contentSub2"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Community of living organisms together with the nonliving components of their environment</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Digital_ecosystem" title="Digital ecosystem">Digital ecosystem</a>.</div>

a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:TopicTOC-Biology" title="Template:TopicTOC-Biology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:TopicTOC-Biology" title="Template talk:TopicTOC-Biology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:TopicTOC-Biology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>An <b>ecosystem</b> (or <b>ecological system</b>) consists of all the organisms and the physical environment with which they interact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapinglossary_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chapinglossary-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 458">: 458 </span></sup> These biotic and <a href="/wiki/Abiotic_component" title="Abiotic component">abiotic components</a> are linked together through nutrient cycles and energy flows. Energy enters the system through <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> and is incorporated into plant tissue. By feeding on plants and on one another, animals play an important role in the movement of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> through the system. They also influence the quantity of plant and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">microbial</a> <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a> present. By breaking down dead <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decomposer" title="Decomposer">decomposers</a> release <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> back to the atmosphere and facilitate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutrient_cycling" title="Nutrient cycling">nutrient cycling</a> by converting nutrients stored in dead biomass back to a form that can be readily used by plants and microbes.
</p><p>Ecosystems are controlled by external and internal <a href="/wiki/Environmental_factor" title="Environmental factor">factors</a>. External factors such as <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parent_material" title="Parent material">parent material</a> which forms the soil and <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a>, control the overall structure of an ecosystem but are not themselves influenced by the ecosystem. Internal factors are controlled, for example, by <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decomposition</a>, root competition, shading, disturbance, succession, and the types of species present. While the <a href="/wiki/Resource_(biology)" title="Resource (biology)">resource</a> inputs are generally controlled by external processes, the availability of these resources within the ecosystem is controlled by internal factors. Therefore, internal factors not only control ecosystem processes but are also controlled by them.
</p><p>Ecosystems are <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dynamic" title="wikt:dynamic">dynamic</a> entities—they are subject to periodic disturbances and are always in the process of recovering from some past disturbance. The tendency of an ecosystem to remain close to its equilibrium state, despite that disturbance, is termed its <a href="/wiki/Resistance_(ecology)" title="Resistance (ecology)">resistance</a>. The capacity of a system to absorb disturbance and reorganize while undergoing change so as to retain essentially the same function, structure, identity, and feedbacks is termed its <a href="/wiki/Ecological_resilience" title="Ecological resilience">ecological resilience</a>. Ecosystems can be studied through a variety of approaches—theoretical studies, studies monitoring specific ecosystems over long periods of time, those that look at differences between ecosystems to elucidate how they work and direct manipulative experimentation. <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">Biomes</a> are general classes or categories of ecosystems. However, there is no clear distinction between biomes and ecosystems. <a href="/wiki/Ecological_classification" title="Ecological classification">Ecosystem classifications</a> are specific kinds of ecological classifications that consider all four elements of the definition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a>: a biotic component, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abiotic" title="Abiotic">abiotic</a> complex, the interactions between and within them, and the physical space they occupy.
</p><p>Ecosystems provide a variety of goods and services upon which people depend. Ecosystem goods include the "tangible, material products" of ecosystem processes such as water, food, fuel, construction material, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medicinal_plant" title="Medicinal plant">medicinal plants</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">Ecosystem services</a>, on the other hand, are generally "improvements in the condition or location of things of value". These include things like the maintenance of <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">hydrological cycles</a>, cleaning air and water, the maintenance of oxygen in the atmosphere, crop <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a> and even things like beauty, inspiration and opportunities for research. Many ecosystems become degraded through human impacts, such as <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">soil loss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Habitat_fragmentation" title="Habitat fragmentation">habitat fragmentation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interbasin_transfer" title="Interbasin transfer">water diversion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wildfire_suppression" title="Wildfire suppression">fire suppression</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced species</a> and <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a>. These threats can lead to abrupt transformation of the ecosystem or to gradual disruption of biotic processes and degradation of <a href="/wiki/Abiotic_component" title="Abiotic component">abiotic</a> conditions of the ecosystem. Once the original ecosystem has lost its defining features, it is considered <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_collapse" title="Ecosystem collapse">"collapsed</a>". <a href="/wiki/Restoration_ecology" title="Restoration ecology">Ecosystem restoration</a> can contribute to achieving the <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a>.
</p>
